        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110782499" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5387293973187923716"><i>Memory</i> is a garden<br/><br/>The rain in his <i>dream</i> was the deepest blue<br/><br/>Nothing can ever be as shocking as life — Except writing<br/><br/>I remember, I remember so as not to forget!<br/><br/>These are the immortal <i>tales</i> I’ve always longed to tell<br/><br/><i>Rüya</i> seemed haunted by the joys and pleasures that had slipped beyond her grasp<br/><br/><i>Galip</i> still felt the terrible <u>eye</u> gazing down at him<br/><br/>Sighs rising and trembling through the timeless air<br/><br/>The life we live is someone else’s dream<br/><br/>There were young people who at certain times in their lives fell</span>
  <span id="freeText5387293973187923716" style="display:none"><i>Memory</i> is a garden<br /><br />The rain in his <i>dream</i> was the deepest blue<br /><br />Nothing can ever be as shocking as life — Except writing<br /><br />I remember, I remember so as not to forget!<br /><br />These are the immortal <i>tales</i> I’ve always longed to tell<br /><br /><i>Rüya</i> seemed haunted by the joys and pleasures that had slipped beyond her grasp<br /><br /><i>Galip</i> still felt the terrible <u>eye</u> gazing down at him<br /><br />Sighs rising and trembling through the timeless air<br /><br />The life we live is someone else’s dream<br /><br />There were young people who at certain times in their lives fell in love simply because of a word, a story, a book they’d both read<br /><br />Everything that had ever been written, even the greatest and most authoritative texts in the world, were about dreams, not real life, dreams conjured up by words<br /><br />Those who have not cracked the secret locked inside our <i>history</i> and our cemeteries cannot presume to speak about us<br /><br />Go out into the street and look at people’s <i>faces</i><br /><br />A song floated across the station, or perhaps from inside the restaurant, a song that spoke of love and grief and the emptiness of life<br /><br />Sometimes snow fell, and sometimes darkness<br /><br />Galip thought how much better it would be if he could leave this world behind forever and live in <i>Celâl’s</i> world instead<br /><br />He has the same sort of life, the same sort of past, the same sort of memories<br /><br />Adorn your stories with bittersweet recollections<br /><br />Celâl’s hiding somewhere inside them. He’s always using his <i>column</i> to send <u>messages</u> to people, all sorts of people — small private messages<br /><br />Life was an endless string of miseries, if one came to an end there was another waiting around the corner<br /><br />This all-knowing, all-seeing eye was gazing down at me now without even trying to conceal itself<br /><br />The eye knew me, and I knew the eye<br /><br />It was I who created the eye, so that it could see me<br /><br />If the eye didn’t see me, I would cease to exist at all<br /><br />We all have a <i>second person</i> buried inside us, a dear friend to whom we whisper to our heart’s content<br /><br />All these buildings, streets, and parks, all these houses laden with a lifetime of memories, reduced to a system of lines and points<br /><br />Crowded collage of people, places, and images from my past<br /><br />I saw myself and my entire life through His eyes<br /><br />Everything that reminds me of you makes me unbearably sad<br /><br />The only way forward was to rip away our memories, our past, our history<br /><br />We should have met long ago<br /><br />I am crazy about mysterious things <br /><br />Instead of bringing them <i>hope</i> You’ve fed them lies. You yourself are <u>Deccal</u><br /><br />His dreams flowing into the stories<br /><br />Everything was a <i>copy</i> of something else<br /><br />The writer spent his nights roaming the city’s dark alleys<br /><br />The melancholy of the rainy streets of <b>Istanbul</b><br /><br />I must be <i>myself</i>, I must be myself, I must be myself<br /><br />All the people I’d had to see that day were still buzzing inside my head<br /><br />Their words, their little noises, their endless stream of demands had blended into a single sound<br /><br />So that midnight, I finally came to see how glad I was to live apart from that madding crowd, from the vile and muddy chaos into which everyone is always commanding me, commanding us all, to immerse ourselves<br /><br />For once I was myself!<br /><br />I must be myself, because if I failed to be myself, I became the person they wanted me to be<br /><br />I was imitating the man who was nothing more than the sum total of all those people I was imitating<br /><br />I look back on those days in search of solace, I am left only with the vague impression of a crowd moving through darkness<br /><br />Our history could only survive <i>underground</i><br /><br />Underground city ultimately wreaking revenge on the overground city<br /><br />How short our lives are, how little we see, how little we know; so let us dream, at least<br /><br />The words lost their meaning and turned into shapes<br /><br />He wished he could remember her with another face, in another story<br /><br />It was perhaps possible to look into the faces of his fellow citizens and see in them the city’s long history — its misfortunes, its lost magnificence, its melancholy and pain<br /><br />They came from a shared defeat, a shared history, a shared shame<br /><br />If he read Celâl’s columns over and over, he would gain access to Celâl’s memory, and once he had infiltrated Celâl’s memory, he would know where he was hiding<br /><br />These people had been able to forget their own sadness by immersing themselves in a story<br /><br />Story that gave meaning to their memories and their melancholy<br /><br />Their sad dreams and sadder memories were fast fading from their minds<br /><br />All murders are copies of other murders, just as all books are copies of other books<br /><br /><i>Creativity</i> rises out of anger, the kind of anger that erases all memory<br /><br />I think of a troubled man pacing up and down the platform of a desolate station in the dead of night, waiting for a train that never comes<br /><br />I shall roam about the city, searching for my beloved, searching for my very past behind every door I open<br /><br />Every object, word, and meaning was now in its proper place, the deeper truth that held them all together was still beyond his reach<br /><br />Somewhere between the lines, he had retreated into the shadows without anyone’s noticing and exchanged his identity<br /><br />The moment arrived when the <i>search</i> itself became more important than the answer he’d come to seek<br /><br />The searcher and the object of his desire changed places, where it was less important to reach a goal than to keep walking toward it<br /><br />Celâl was obsessed with the little tricks, ambiguities, and fictions that allowed him to manipulate others from a distance<br /><br />From now on I shall devote myself utterly to the hidden poetry of our faces, the terrifying secret that lurks inside our human gaze<br /><br />This was a dangerous game he’d been dragged into, a deadly trap<br /><br /><i>Books</i> were always telling us, everything was connected to everything else<br /><br />These faces Celâl had been collecting for thirty years might offer him glimpses of this other realm to which he longed to escape<br /><br />Faces that might once have spoken of pain, misery, and melancholy now said nothing<br /><br />He began to think there might be a link between the mystery of letters and the meanings in faces<br /><br />Every object that surrounds you is hiding a secret<br /><br />Everyone was impersonating someone else and everything was a replica of an absent original<br /><br />Behind every tree were letters, gruesome, bloodcurdling letters<br /><br />Our faces had emptied of all meaning, and with it, the art of reading faces<br /><br />Our eyebrows, our eyes, our noses, our gazes, our expressions, our faces were blank<br /><br />There were an infinite number of possible interpretations of any given text<br /><br />It was like an unending maze of city streets, with each street leading to another: maps resembling human faces<br /><br />The more he discovers, the more the <i>mystery</i> spreads<br /><br />The smell brought back memories of the days he and Rüya had spent in this apartment<br /><br />They were all made from this smell<br /><br />I gazed into the mirror and read my face. I dreamed that I had at last become the person I’ve always longed to become<br /><br />He felt like a detective who had just found the key to a mystery, who would now be using the same key to open new doors<br /><br />If you want to turn your world upside down, all you have to do is somehow convince yourself you might be someone else<br /><br />You’re a flower in the garden of my memory<br /><br />Istanbul nights are endless, a ghost with a guilty conscience cannot sleep<br /><br />I believed in a world without heroes<br /><br />I could never convince you to be content with an ordinary life<br /><br />It was he who had changed and not the city<br /><br />Istanbul was an open book to him now, it harbored no secrets<br /><br />Was this life repeating itself?<br /><br />First voice is the simple persona, the voice you’ll use with anyone<br /><br />Second voice belongs to the man you’d like to be, the mask you’ve stolen from those you most admire<br /><br />Third voice is the dark self, the dark style<br /><br />That cities are made from addresses, addresses from letters, and letters from faces<br /><br />To read was to gaze into a mirror<br /><br />Those who know the secret behind the looking glass are able to travel to the other side<br /><br />You are possessed of a mighty pen, a pen that can realize all these dreams and astonishing memories<br /><br />At least once in his life, a writer should have a chance to meet his <i>perfect reader</i><br /><br />We do not have <i>private</i> lives in this country<br /><br />Mystery is sovereign, so treat it with respect<br /><br />You love me. You loved me with all your heart. Everything you wrote, you wrote to me<br /><br />You talked about my cherry lips and my crescent eyebrows, all this time I’ve been the one inspiring you<br /><br />The line between us faded into the mists of my imagination, and I could no longer see where you ended and I began<br /><br />No one can ever be himself<br /><br />I am both myself and someone else<br /><br />I followed him all over Istanbul like a shadow<br /><br />To live in an oppressed, defeated country is to be someone else<br /><br />I am someone else, therefore I am<br /><br />But what if this person I want to become is himself someone else? This is the crux, the heart of the deception<br /><br />The stories seem to write themselves. They flow by their own logic<br /><br />For the pages that follow — the <i>black pages</i> — are the memoirs of a sleepwalker<br /><br />Tears. Silence. The noises of a strange house<br /><br />Because nothing is as surprising as life — Except for writing<br /><br />Except for writing, the only consolation</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3457888645?book_show_action=true">Aug 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Newton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Newton" href="/user/show/19540749-richard-newton">Richard Newton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3457888645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1030851367246202316">This booked just squeezed five stars for me. It is not perfect, but it is an interesting and well written book. <br/><br/>What is it about? Well there is a superficial story and you can read it just for that story - the mystery Galip tries to unravel when he wants to find where his missing wife and older cousin have gone. It does intrigue and at times is a page turner, but it is too odd at times to really read for that story alone. Below this it seems to me to be about many things - about writing and bein</span>
  <span id="freeText1030851367246202316" style="display:none">This booked just squeezed five stars for me. It is not perfect, but it is an interesting and well written book. <br /><br />What is it about? Well there is a superficial story and you can read it just for that story - the mystery Galip tries to unravel when he wants to find where his missing wife and older cousin have gone. It does intrigue and at times is a page turner, but it is too odd at times to really read for that story alone. Below this it seems to me to be about many things - about writing and being a writer, about love and family, about Turkey balancing between being an Eastern and a Western country, it is about memory and it is about personality - who we really are. I am sure you can find more things to review in this. <br /><br />It is not an easy read to begin with. It is worth reading the translators note to understand the complexity of translating from Turkish into English, so no "literal" translation is going to work. It will always need interpretation. The translator has done a great job as you are never overly conscious you are reading a translation. But Turkish is complex and this means the first 50 pages will probably dissuade many people from going further. I found once I'd got passed these I got into the style of the book and it became a fairly straight forward read - even if like a book by an esoteric sect, you can find layers of meaning here. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1030851367246202316" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_49675045" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/49675045" />
    <a title="Christopher" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2140237-christopher"><img alt="Christopher" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1250386318p2/2140237.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/49675045?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Christopher" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Christopher" href="/user/show/2140237-christopher">Christopher</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer49675045" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17574146358747562480">While reading Orhan Pamuk's breakthrough novel, it is easy to feel as lost as the central character, a lawyer who discovers that the central mystery is not the whereabouts in enigmatic Istanbul of his missing wife, but rather that of identity itself. His identity, that of a newspaper columnist given to revolutionary tales and historical asides, that of a mysterious caller, and in fact, of Istanbul itself and its relation to the culture and identity of the West are all called into question.<br/><br/>The wr</span>
  <span id="freeText17574146358747562480" style="display:none">While reading Orhan Pamuk's breakthrough novel, it is easy to feel as lost as the central character, a lawyer who discovers that the central mystery is not the whereabouts in enigmatic Istanbul of his missing wife, but rather that of identity itself. His identity, that of a newspaper columnist given to revolutionary tales and historical asides, that of a mysterious caller, and in fact, of Istanbul itself and its relation to the culture and identity of the West are all called into question.<br /><br />The writing is not dense, in fact, the translation is in turns poetically sinuous and rigidly straightforward. Every other chapter replicates a newspaper column written by a friend of Galip's, and the brother of his wife, who becomes integral to the question of identity in the story. These columns cover a wide array of subject matter, from historical local legends of gangsters and their exploits to deeply introspective examinations of the mystery of life itself.<br /><br />These column chapters help break up the tedium of the first half of the central narrative, which plods on ponderously after Galip in search of his wife. It is only in the second half that I looked eagerly forward to the narrative chapters, wishing the column chapters would end sooner.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17574146358747562480" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_91591684" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91591684" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/978026-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1251925098p2/978026.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/91591684?book_show_action=true">Feb 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/978026-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780156003292" href="/book/show/1060345.The_Black_Book">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer91591684" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3782739155087315216">I picked up this book at a library book sale - in part for the picture of the Hagia Sophia on the cover, the blurbs ("tantalizing," "splendid," "delicious"), and the promise of the exotic in Istanbul. The copy I purchased was published before Pamuk won the Nobel Prize.<br/><br/>This is an intricate and beautifully written book. It is a detective story like Calvino's If On A Winter's Night a Traveller is a detective story. Like that story it is a reflection on writing and identity, but set in Istanbul with</span>
  <span id="freeText3782739155087315216" style="display:none">I picked up this book at a library book sale - in part for the picture of the Hagia Sophia on the cover, the blurbs ("tantalizing," "splendid," "delicious"), and the promise of the exotic in Istanbul. The copy I purchased was published before Pamuk won the Nobel Prize.<br /><br />This is an intricate and beautifully written book. It is a detective story like Calvino's If On A Winter's Night a Traveller is a detective story. Like that story it is a reflection on writing and identity, but set in Istanbul with hints of Rumi, Sufi mysteries, and the Arabian Nights (as well as many more mundane details). Galip is searching for his missing wife, but also for himself, and seems to think he will find each with his wife's half-brother, a famous newspaper columnist. The journalist's articles form alternate chapters and dig into Istanbul, its history and stories, and the nature of identity in a Turkey inundated with Western images. At times funny, annoying, and heart breakingly sad, the chapters of this book took considerable time and attention to read (it required I be alert, not bedtime reading this). But its imagery, stories, and maybe its ideas, will be with me for awhile. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3782739155087315216" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72341080-bob-newman?shelf=turkey-and-lit">turkey-and-lit</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2309768999" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15954085674826837061">Plotless in Istanbul,---but intriguing nonetheless<br/><br/>Nobody could say THE BLACK BOOK is a thriller, but it is thrilling writing. An Istanbul lawyer's wife disappears. A related columnist also disappears. The lawyer looks for them. That's about it. But the search and the thinking is the thing. Pamuk's style blends Proust with Borges. If you find that intriguing, read the book. Pamuk manages to combine intimate details of life in the modern city of Istanbul with tales of Sufi masters, long ago execut</span>
  <span id="freeText15954085674826837061" style="display:none">Plotless in Istanbul,---but intriguing nonetheless<br /><br />Nobody could say THE BLACK BOOK is a thriller, but it is thrilling writing. An Istanbul lawyer's wife disappears. A related columnist also disappears. The lawyer looks for them. That's about it. But the search and the thinking is the thing. Pamuk's style blends Proust with Borges. If you find that intriguing, read the book. Pamuk manages to combine intimate details of life in the modern city of Istanbul with tales of Sufi masters, long ago executioners, Ottoman pashas, and underground fantasies with a great deal of soul-searching on the nature of human identity. "I want to be somebody else, therefore I am" is his theme again, following on from his previous work, "The White Castle". Dreams, intertwining identities, the connection between writing and life, even cryptograms. This is fascinating stuff. Though sometimes the book lags, it always picks up again with another strange twist. Pamuk is certainly one of the most interesting writers working today.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15954085674826837061" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer13443369055685810580">What can I say? I loved it at the beginning, but then it became so repetitive, so illogical... Galip could have been some of the deepest characters ever, but there are moments he seems so dummy... The issue of "being someone else instead of being one's self" is really deep and interesting, but although the story is brilliantly written, it never got to the point of being a book you can't put down.   <span id="freeText13443369055685810580" style="display:none">What can I say? I loved it at the beginning, but then it became so repetitive, so illogical... Galip could have been some of the deepest characters ever, but there are moments he seems so dummy... The issue of "being someone else instead of being one's self" is really deep and interesting, but although the story is brilliantly written, it never got to the point of being a book you can't put down. In fact, putting it down was sometimes a relief! Finishing it actually became a challenge for me! Just as Ruya and Celal did, the mistery of the plot vanished amidst all these chapters about everybody wondering how to be themselves in Istanbul. Thank God, the end is good, surprising, unexpected, and the last chapter is fast and to the point, although it also has some deep thoughts. I cannot say it's a bad book, but it is certainly hard to read. I'm not sure I will read another book from Pamuk. Probably I will give him a second try...</span>

<div id="review_69360230" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/69360230" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/566226-john"><img alt="John" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer69360230" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17025874687308627776">This is the best Pamuk novel I've read, and it is the one that made his reputation in Turkey. It was not as widely-known to English-language readers as two of his subsequent novels ("My Name Is Red" and "Snow") because of a more difficult translation published in 1995. This newer translation is by Pamuk's close English-language collaborator Maureen Freely, and was published in 2006 shortly before Pamuk won the Nobel Prize. <br/><br/>The setting is Istanbul shortly before the military coup of 1980, though </span>
  <span id="freeText17025874687308627776" style="display:none">This is the best Pamuk novel I've read, and it is the one that made his reputation in Turkey. It was not as widely-known to English-language readers as two of his subsequent novels ("My Name Is Red" and "Snow") because of a more difficult translation published in 1995. This newer translation is by Pamuk's close English-language collaborator Maureen Freely, and was published in 2006 shortly before Pamuk won the Nobel Prize. <br /><br />The setting is Istanbul shortly before the military coup of 1980, though the political situation is a mere backdrop for an intensely personal story. (In fact, if you're looking for any political thrills, you're not going to get it, since as in "Snow" Pamuk's characters are disaffected and discouraged ex-leftists at best.) There are layers of hypnotic stories about Istanbul here that are best read directly rather than described. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17025874687308627776" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_62134705" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/62134705" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2487874-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1246737947p2/2487874.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/62134705?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/2487874-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer62134705" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4619007711159702338">This is a massive achievement. It's quite exhausting to read as the author throws stories, characters, similies at us at a very rapid rate.<br/>What is so special about it is the way he works on several levels: he brings home what it is like to be Turkish, how Istanbul is the frontier of cultures, and how much history is there. But on another, more modernist level, he raises questions of what it is to be an author, the relationship between reader and writer, and ultimately, what defines our identity.</span>
  <span id="freeText4619007711159702338" style="display:none">This is a massive achievement. It's quite exhausting to read as the author throws stories, characters, similies at us at a very rapid rate.<br />What is so special about it is the way he works on several levels: he brings home what it is like to be Turkish, how Istanbul is the frontier of cultures, and how much history is there. But on another, more modernist level, he raises questions of what it is to be an author, the relationship between reader and writer, and ultimately, what defines our identity.<br />Only criticism - I felt it could have been a bit shorter - my copy is 460 pages, and honestly I think he has communicated everything in the first 350.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4619007711159702338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2296083078" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2296083078" />
    <a title="Simona" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11699787-simona"><img alt="Simona" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495730456p2/11699787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2296083078?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Simona" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Simona" href="/user/show/11699787-simona">Simona</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11699787-simona?shelf=review">review</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2296083078" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12760020763329427122">The story is placed in a rather simple frame - a man looking for a missing wife and uncle. From here on, things are a little more complicated ... the story (well, many of them) is complex and very dense at all levels. A rich, picturesque, detailed language with which author paints an atmosphere filled with sadness, melancholy in individuals and in the city - Istanbul (which is a completely independent character) ...and all of them are in a constant search for identity - personal, and through ind</span>
  <span id="freeText12760020763329427122" style="display:none">The story is placed in a rather simple frame - a man looking for a missing wife and uncle. From here on, things are a little more complicated ... the story (well, many of them) is complex and very dense at all levels. A rich, picturesque, detailed language with which author paints an atmosphere filled with sadness, melancholy in individuals and in the city - Istanbul (which is a completely independent character) ...and all of them are in a constant search for identity - personal, and through individuals identity of the city and wider - states. The metaphysical questions/searches of an individual are deepened with a wider context through stories (in the form of columns) that capture history, religion, politics, nationality, and of course the collision of the East with the West.<br /><br />The story has a very dynamic, easy to follow structure - the narration of the abandoned man in alternation with the columns written by the disappeared uncle, which allows fluent (and clear) change between present and the past, but becouse of the richness and density of the ideas, this book requires devoted reader in excelletn reading form.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12760020763329427122" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_19063342" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19063342" />
    <a title="Martin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1038095-martin"><img alt="Martin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1368629647p2/1038095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19063342?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Martin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Martin" href="/user/show/1038095-martin">Martin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19063342" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1538292074401290684">This was the first book I have read by Orhan Pamuk...apparently, it is not his best known...described as a "cult classic" by the Times Of London...and it appears to have come into translation much later than his other works. The story is s a rather bizarre "mystery" focusing on themes of identity, loss and isolation, amongst other things...and while Pamuk is obviously an amazing stylist (they don't tend to give out Noble Prizes willy-nilly), this particular book seems to have lost quite a bit in</span>
  <span id="freeText1538292074401290684" style="display:none">This was the first book I have read by Orhan Pamuk...apparently, it is not his best known...described as a "cult classic" by the Times Of London...and it appears to have come into translation much later than his other works. The story is s a rather bizarre "mystery" focusing on themes of identity, loss and isolation, amongst other things...and while Pamuk is obviously an amazing stylist (they don't tend to give out Noble Prizes willy-nilly), this particular book seems to have lost quite a bit in the translation...I suspect it would it would have been much more engaging had I been Turkish (lots of references to Turkish history, and specific references to modern day and historical Istanbul) and perhaps reading it in it's native language...there is even a translator's afterword that explains some of the difficulties of the translation, which would have served much better as a forward; setting the template so to speak...while I enjoyed the book overall, I was never completely hooked by it, and even found myself losing interest in the plots and the characters about 2/3 of the way through...though things did pick up a bit and seemed to come together in a much more engaging way towards the end. If my calculations are correct this was Pamuk's first novel, and frankly it reads like one. But it definitely intrigued me enough to check out more of his work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1538292074401290684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1901668023" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1901668023" />
    <a title="Nihal Vrana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18959010-nihal-vrana"><img alt="Nihal Vrana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1364813084p2/18959010.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1901668023?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nihal Vrana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nihal Vrana" href="/user/show/18959010-nihal-vrana">Nihal Vrana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7112443-kara-kitap">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1901668023" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15132572442667401794">This book did not say anything to me; it just washed over me. Maybe because it is too familiar; you need to be not-from-Turkey to be able to see it as something deep and mysterious. Although, I am hard-pressed to understand how you can visualize anything with the meager descriptions in the book. I mean I actually know the places you know; and it was even hard for me. Same thing goes for the characters; I literally know at least one person like each character and still all of them were forgettabl</span>
  <span id="freeText15132572442667401794" style="display:none">This book did not say anything to me; it just washed over me. Maybe because it is too familiar; you need to be not-from-Turkey to be able to see it as something deep and mysterious. Although, I am hard-pressed to understand how you can visualize anything with the meager descriptions in the book. I mean I actually know the places you know; and it was even hard for me. Same thing goes for the characters; I literally know at least one person like each character and still all of them were forgettable and two-dimensional.<br /><br />Besides, the whole tone of the book is whiny while trying to be existential. It ascribes to describe a certain emptiness/bleakness in the life of low-middle class Turks; but for me it only shows an inability to see the richness of the material he is treating on the writer's part.<br /><br />Maybe the only part I enjoyed in the book is that it captured the psychotic relationship between journal columnists and their audience in Turkey. After reading this book, you can understand why so many columnist in Turkey are being arrested now. Somehow, weirdly, they are very influential.<br /><br />I was happy that Pamuk won the Nobel Prize; but for me the real gems of Turkish literature are Oguz Atay and Ahmet Hamdi Tanpinar. Try those if they are available in your language.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15132572442667401794" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('MCLgg00eDh2kBqI4hitR1kUFsw5lc0B2U70F1xXOR4l7jOndpFgwGmjRr2sEPb7B2bB/ZyANEYdvigml/C40AA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8EQFnmS4dDF6rCTZWm7ctrHuPDv24bsfmjDNVn2wvSe76gzAjf5KNrZ7KYrYeDOhLVvwUrOf6u6mB8EklFDOrg==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1728.Orhan_Pamuk">About Orhan Pamuk</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1728.Orhan_Pamuk"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1423469681p3/1728.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1728.Orhan_Pamuk">Orhan Pamuk
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
7,730
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer557172431443932587">Orhan Pamuk was born in Istanbul in 1952 and grew up in a large family similar to those which he describes in his novels Cevdet Bey and His Sons and The Black Book, in the wealthy westernised district of Nisantasi. As he writes in his autobiographical book Istanbul, from his childhood until the age of 22 he devoted himself largely to painting and dreamed of becoming an artist. After graduating fro</span>
  <span id="freeText557172431443932587" style="display:none">Orhan Pamuk was born in Istanbul in 1952 and grew up in a large family similar to those which he describes in his novels Cevdet Bey and His Sons and The Black Book, in the wealthy westernised district of Nisantasi. As he writes in his autobiographical book Istanbul, from his childhood until the age of 22 he devoted himself largely to painting and dreamed of becoming an artist. After graduating from the secular American Robert College in Istanbul, he studied architecture at Istanbul Technical University for three years, but abandoned the course when he gave up his ambition to become an architect and artist. He went on to graduate in journalism from Istanbul University, but never worked as a journalist. At the age of 23 Pamuk decided to become a novelist, and giving up everything else retreated into his flat and began to write.<br /><br />His first novel <i>Cevdet Bey and His Sons</i> was published seven years later in 1982. The novel is the story of three generations of a wealthy Istanbul family living in Nisantasi, Pamuk's own home district. The novel was awarded both the Orhan Kemal and Milliyet literary prizes. The following year Pamuk published his novel <i>The Silent House</i>, which in French translation won the 1991 Prix de la découverte européene. <i>The White Castle</i> (1985) about the frictions and friendship between a Venetian slave and an Ottoman scholar was published in English and many other languages from 1990 onwards, bringing Pamuk his first international fame. The same year Pamuk went to America, where he was a visiting scholar at Columbia University in New York from 1985 to 1988. It was there that he wrote most of his novel <i>The Black Book</i>, in which the streets, past, chemistry and texture of Istanbul are described through the story of a lawyer seeking his missing wife. This novel was published in Turkey in 1990, and the French translation won the Prix France Culture. <i>The Black Book</i> enlarged Pamuk's fame both in Turkey and internationally as an author at once popular and experimental, and able to write about past and present with the same intensity. In 1991 Pamuk's daughter Rüya was born. That year saw the production of a film Hidden Face, whose script by Pamuk was based on a one-page story in <i>The Black Book</i>.<br /><br />His novel <i>The New Life</i>, about young university students influenced by a mysterious book, was published in Turkey in 1994 and became one of the most widely read books in Turkish literature. <i>My Name Is Red</i>, about Ottoman and Persian artists and their ways of seeing and portraying the non-western world, told through a love story and family story, was published in 1998. This novel won the French Prix du meilleur livre étranger, the Italian Grinzane Cavour (2002) and the International IMPAC Dublin literary award (2003). From the mid-1990s Pamuk took a critical stance towards the Turkish state in articles about human rights and freedom of thought, although he took little interest in politics. <i>Snow</i>, which he describes as “my first and last political novel” was published in 2002. In this book set in the small city of Kars in northeastern Turkey he experimented with a new type of “political novel”, telling the story of violence and tension between political Islamists, soldiers, secularists, and Kurdish and Turkish nationalists. <i>Snow</i> was selected as one of the best 100 books of 2004 by The New York Times. In 1999 a selection of his articles on literature and culture written for newspapers and magazines in Turkey and abroad, together with a selection of writings from his private notebooks, was published under the title <i>Other Colours</i>. Pamuk's most recent book, Istanbul, is a poetical work that is hard to classify, combining the author's early memoirs up to the age of 22, and an essay about the city of Istanbul, illustrated with photographs from his own album, and pictures by western painters and Turkish photographers.</span>
  <a data-text-id="557172431443932587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;When you look into the faces of these quiet creatures who don't know how to tell stories--who are mute, who can't make themselves heard, who fade into the woodwork, who only think of the perfect answer after the fact, after they're back at home, who can never think of a story that anyone else will find interesting--is there not more depth and more meaning in them? You can see every letter of every untold story swimming on their faces, and all the signs of silence, dejection, and even defeat. You can even imagine your own face in those faces, can't you?&rdquo;</span>
